  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/628284-i-have-a-hard-time-what-books-are-in-order">I have a hard time what books are in order or books that are related, which ones to read first so that the stories make sense. Is there a place that show books in order? I am finished with this book, where should I go from here?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/34363051-quiddity"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/34363051-quiddity">Quiddity</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I have read all of his books since the 80's in order of release. I started reading him under the name the Leigh Nichols and then went back to read som<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I have read all of his books since the 80's in order of release. I started reading him under the name the Leigh Nichols and then went back to read some of his books released in the 70's to catch up. If you liked Twilight eyes, I'd recommend going back from the late 70's onwards through his 'Leigh Nichols' period, if not before. He has a sea change in his writing around the late 90's that steps away from the genre I found most compelling to read by him, but as a favourite author for most of my life - I've followed him through the last 15 years from loyalty and even if his stories don't connect quite as deeply with me, his wonderful writing style still does. Reading chronologically works well with Koontz as over time his plots and characters become more numerous and involved and working backwards from his current writing may leave you a little disappointed. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_185316399" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185316399" />
    <a title="The Pirate Ghost (Formerly known as the Curmudgeon)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon"><img alt="The Pirate Ghost (Formerly known as the Curmudgeon)" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185316399?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Pirate Ghost (Formerly known as the Curmudgeon)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Pirate Ghost (Formerly known as the Curmudgeon)" href="/user/show/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon">The Pirate Ghost (Formerly known as the Curmudgeon)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon?shelf=favorite-reads">favorite-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon?shelf=good-read">good-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon?shelf=really-good-read">really-good-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5555043-the-pirate-ghost-formerly-known-as-the-curmudgeon?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer185316399&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185316399" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14897143645330853586">Warning: there may be spoilers here and Caution: that last real post on this thread was long and rambling, this one is likely to be worse. <br/><br/>Why Twilight eyes? I mean, I frequently find that even amongst Kootz fans when I say this is my favorite, I get people turning heads and going "huh?" <br/><br/>Let me explain what about that book affected me. <br/><br/>The first thing that I picked up on were pieces like this worked into the opening of many chapters.<br/><br/><i>"Every hour the radio news told us of Kitty Genovese, who had b</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14897143645330853586" style="display:none">Warning: there may be spoilers here and Caution: that last real post on this thread was long and rambling, this one is likely to be worse. <br /><br />Why Twilight eyes? I mean, I frequently find that even amongst Kootz fans when I say this is my favorite, I get people turning heads and going "huh?" <br /><br />Let me explain what about that book affected me. <br /><br />The first thing that I picked up on were pieces like this worked into the opening of many chapters.<br /><br /><i>"Every hour the radio news told us of Kitty Genovese, who had been killed two days ago. 38 of her Kew Gardens neighbors had heard her terrified calls for help and watched as her attacker stabbed her, crept away, then returned to stab her again, finally killing on her own doorstep. None of the thirty-eight had gone to her aid. None called the police until a half hour after Kitty was dead. Two days later the story was still at the top of the news, and the whole country was trying to understand what the nightmarish events in Kew Gardens said about the inhumanity, callousness and isolation of urban man and woman. <br /><br />"We just didn't want to get involved," the thirty eight onlookers said, as if being of the same speicies, and age, and society of Kitty Genovese, was not involvement enough to illicit mercy and compassion…." </i><br /><br />The story is set around (just after) the assassination of President Kennedy (63/64). I doubt there was any intent on Koontz's part to artistically mirror another work of art in another media but this bit stuck in my memory and I can even recall it now almost verbatim. He did not invent this story <br />(<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.trutv.com/library/crime/serial_killers/predators/kitty_genovese/1.html">http://www.trutv.com/library/crime/se...</a>) <br /><br />The Heroine Rya's words to the hero here are just as haunting.<br /><br /><i> "Not all of the evil in the world comes from the goblins." </i><br /><br />It reminded me of Paul Simon and Art Garfunkel's "Silent Night." They recorded a wonderful version of the haunting, yet beautiful Christmas Carol with The Seven O'clock news playing in the background. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=63u8T3abETo">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=63u8T3...</a> <br /><br /><i>"Not all of the evil in the world comes from the goblins."</i><br /><br /> I cannot read this part of Twilight eyes, or listen to The Simon and Garfunkel's Silent Night without recalling the other. <br /><br />The same year I read this, I was stationed on the USS Mount Baker AE34, an ammunitions carrier. We'd just worked our butts off bringing beans bullets and gas to the fleet during the Libyan Crisis. Our deployment had been extended to past 8 months instead of the usual 6, and we had pulled into Rota Spain as the last port before returning to Charleston S.C., our home port. A sailor, off of the USS Jessie L. Brown, a Charleston Based Frigate returning with us, had come back to the ship the last night we were in port, drunk, fell off of the brow between the pier and the ship and drown before they could get him out of the water leaving a wife and two children behind. <br /><br /><i>"Not all of the evil in the world is caused by the Goblins." </i><br /><br />In the book, the character "Jolly" a kindly man, nothing like the sailor off of the Brown save the senselessness of his death, was found dead on the merry go round. He wanted nothing in life but to bring joy to children and shared a child like infatuation with the ride. <br /><br />The Goblins did this one. <br /><br />My point is, that Koontz, without trying, had put words to that dark side of humanity and fate that nobody ever wants to acknowledge, but can't ignore, the object of our denial, <i>"all men are created equal and endowed by their creator certain inalienable rights,"</i> we are equally capable of looking away instead of showing compassion and mercy and getting involved. Even then, sometimes <i>”shit happens. </i><br /><br />That is another way of saying that not all of the bad things in life are caused by madmen like the crazed gunman in Phoenix who confronted Gabrielle Giffords. Anyone of us could have been Kitty Genovese? <br /><br />You think I'm being over dramatic? <br /><br />What about so many teens and young adults, driven to suicide or tragedy by bullies who teased them about being Gay, or picked on them because of who they liked as a boyfriend? IN HIGH SCHOOL! How many people, good teachers, good kids, good parents knew this was harassment was going on and did not want to get involved? <br /><br /><i>"Not all of the evil in the world is caused by the Goblins." </i><br /><br />Looking away is sometimes as evil as perpetrating the act yourself. The gunman in Phoenix is legitimately crazy he needs and deserves proper medical attention as much as he needs to be held accountable for the acts that he committed. What's everyone else's excuse?<br /><br />This dark side of our humanity is clearly exposed in Koontz early writing, particularly in Twilight eyes if you care to see it. In one discussion group people derided Koontz because he left people with a sense of hope in his writing. I appreciate that sense of hope. <br /><br />By exposing us to ourselves, or our true nature, we can become aware and make choices, and, that's the first step to changing how we live. Are we really better off being left with Kitty Genovese and no hope of evolving to a higher form of ourselves? <br /><br />Don't get me wrong. I'm not sure Koontz knew what he was putting in his early work. I bet he really just wanted to keep the royalty checks coming I'm simply glad it was there for me to see. <br /><br />Good book, fast read, solid characters and maybe something else to think about, if you are willing to look for it. <br /><br />There is more truth in fiction, than in most history books when it comes to saying what really needs to be said. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14897143645330853586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_624049965" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/624049965" />
    <a title="Gerald" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18658199-gerald"><img alt="Gerald" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367441277p2/18658199.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/624049965?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gerald" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gerald" href="/user/show/18658199-gerald">Gerald</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/9586103-twilight-eyes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer624049965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1515194088981865549">A masterpiece.<br/><br/>Dean Koontz has written many great stories, but this may be his best ever. A haunting, chilling tale, following the journey of Slim MacKenzie. He's a drifter, but he's no ordinary man. With eyes the color of twilight, he's been blessed with a psychic gift: premonitions. He's also been cursed, for Slim can see the monsters hiding among us, feeding on our suffering.<br/><br/>And when Slim joins a traveling carnival seeking sanctuary, what he'll find is a hunting ground-with humanity as the pre</span>
  <span id="freeText1515194088981865549" style="display:none">A masterpiece.<br /><br />Dean Koontz has written many great stories, but this may be his best ever. A haunting, chilling tale, following the journey of Slim MacKenzie. He's a drifter, but he's no ordinary man. With eyes the color of twilight, he's been blessed with a psychic gift: premonitions. He's also been cursed, for Slim can see the monsters hiding among us, feeding on our suffering.<br /><br />And when Slim joins a traveling carnival seeking sanctuary, what he'll find is a hunting ground-with humanity as the prey.<br /><br />What makes it even more compelling, are what he sees, the "goblins" hiding inside, the monsters wearing the perfect disguises, looking and sounding all too human, invisible to us, but not to him. And, look around, at some of the horrible things done to people by other people, it makes the idea of "goblins" hiding inside, all too real. Fiction, yes, but you never know.<br /><br />This is a keep you up late, page turner, can't put it down kind of book. You'll be wrung out by the power of the story, and the characters who struggle against the monsters hiding inside.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1515194088981865549" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_110319375" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110319375" />
    <a title="CasualDebris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/268040-casualdebris"><img alt="CasualDebris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330729470p2/268040.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110319375?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="CasualDebris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="CasualDebris" href="/user/show/268040-casualdebris">CasualDebris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/268040-casualdebris?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110319375" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11363309626240489323">For my extended review and a parody of the writing, please visit <a href='http://casualdebris.blogspot.ca/2010/07/twilight-eyes-ny-berkley-books-1987.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Casual Debris</a>.<br/><br/>Twilight Eyes fails on every level: conception, plot, character, development, character development, setting delineation and writing. Even the title is weak: a misplaced 1980s pop tune. The novel focuses on a seventeen year-old boy who has the inexplicable natural ability to see through the disguises of certain "people" and recognize them for what they truly are: porcine creatures bent on exterminating the human race,</span>
  <span id="freeText11363309626240489323" style="display:none">For my extended review and a parody of the writing, please visit <a href="http://casualdebris.blogspot.ca/2010/07/twilight-eyes-ny-berkley-books-1987.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Casual Debris</a>.<br /><br />Twilight Eyes fails on every level: conception, plot, character, development, character development, setting delineation and writing. Even the title is weak: a misplaced 1980s pop tune. The novel focuses on a seventeen year-old boy who has the inexplicable natural ability to see through the disguises of certain "people" and recognize them for what they truly are: porcine creatures bent on exterminating the human race, driven by their predisposed hatred of humankind. The boy, Slim MacKenzie (as he has aliased himself), is on a journey to destroy these evil creatures which he randomly refers to as "goblins." The novel opens with Slim sneaking onto the closed lot of a travelling carnival, which is the setting for the first half of the novel. The second half of the novel is set in a small town that has become a hive for these monsters.<br /><br />The narrator of his own story, Slim MacKenzie is a seventeen year-old drifter from Oregon, who is athletic, sensitive, morally upright and older than his years, traits that we are constantly being reminded of as though Koontz is trying hard to convince us of their accuracy. But as Slim sees through the goblin mask, I can see through Koontz and am blatantly aware that Slim is instead uninteresting and unbelievable, as flat as his prose and with less charm than the ink that was wasted in printing the text. The narration itself is immediately marred by the fact that the narrator is ageless, seventeen or a hundred and two, leading me to suspect that it is not Slim himself narrating but someone pretending to be him, and I am left with the notion that Koontz has merely immersed himself in what is essentially a juvenile male-driven fantasy.<br /><br />Throughout my reading I kept wondering about narrator Slim's vantage point and his motive in telling the story. The events occur in 1963, but it is unclear at what stage in his life Slim is currently in and how distanced he has become from the events he is relating. The voice is ageless and remote, trance-like and devoid of personality, not seventeen but neither forty, which is likely what impels Koontz to keep reminding the reader that Slim is only seventeen. Koontz takes it for granted that this is even an issue, but while we don't require actual details of Slim's present circumstances, we do need to be somewhat grounded with narrator and narrative. The story should have been written in the third person. This would have eliminated the need for the grounding that Koontz is unable to deliver, and would have made Slim so much more interesting. I believe Koontz chose to write the story in the first person in order to allow for some dull moralizing that weighs the book down as heavily as a building would sink a rubber dinghy.<br /><br />With such an elusive narrator we can only guess as to what inspired Slim to tell his story. The reader is expected to believe the narrator's every word; Slim doesn't even attempt to convince us that these goblins are real, and he proceeds with the presumption that we automatically believe him. Moreover, he is not trying to warn us of the danger of these hell-bent goblins, as he tells his story in a fairly casual way, withholding key pieces of information and revealing them at seemingly random points of the narrative. Slim is not even focused on these goblins and their threat to humanity, as he wades in a swamp of unimportant particulars. The emphasis on the most personal details of his sexual relationship with lover Rya Raines leads me to question his sensitive and moral nature, for he ends up coming off as an immature and overly-sexed man-child, bragging about giving Rya two orgasms before he even enters her, gushing embarrassingly over her perfectly rounded breasts, and then describing in odd detail his own orgasms: "through the medium of my sperm I passed my own heartbeat into her, the two now thumping as one." (p. 143) Perhaps this description is supposed to contrast the "spurting" blood of the goblins in the following paragraph, with "its thick warm jets of thick crimson serum," the serum in contrast with the semen, one giving life while the other steals it away (though this fails not only because of awful execution, but because Rya cannot have children and hence the life-giving aspect is moot). I don't believe any contrasts are attempted here; it is all part of a juvenile male fantasy.<br /><br />Story-wise very little happens. Over the course of 451 pages we are given very little in the way of story and plot, with a rambling narrative that lacks direction. Instead of story we have naïve Christian moralizing and philosophizing (I don't mean that Christian moralizing and philosophizing itself is naïve, just that Koontz's own practice of it is less than insightful). Throughout the narrative Koontz/Slim reminds us that some people are good, while others are bad. Some are so bad that they may as well be evil "goblins," though overall humankind is filled with more good than bad and we should not harm the good because there is some bad in the flock. Destroy bad and maintain good; such is the purpose of life. Koontz tries to add ambiguity by illustrating extreme scenarios of "real" humans who act as though they are goblins, trying to drown us with the notion that the creatures may have a valid point in their desire to destroy humanity.<br /><br />Furthermore, just like these goblins some "real" humans act friendly but are manifestations of evil and wear their friendliness as a disguise to allow them to perpetrate more acts of evil. These attempts at uniting story with base morality fall flat, as though Koontz was desperate to add some other dimension to the text in order to save it from its inherent uselessness. Amid this mess Koontz repeatedly uses Christian imagery or reference in everything from his similes and metaphors to the moralizing itself. Slim hears a scream that sounds like the voice of God (is it not sinful to assume that a mere man can imagine the voice of God?), and my personal favourite, Slim's statement near the end of Part One that love is the cross on which he was crucified. Each page is seemingly filled with such allusions that the practice is quickly tiring, and eventually more than irritating.<br /><br />The novel is written with an agonizingly grating stream of repetition. Not only do scenes repeat themselves, but descriptions from death to sex are essentially reformatted every few chapters. We are plagued by constant repetition of how evil these "goblins" are, beaten over the head with overused adjectives such as "evil," "dark," and so forth, and are told over and over when and where Slim and Rya make love, and just how his semen intermingles with Rya's inner self, or some such nonsense.<br /><br />This repetition is not reserved for descriptions and scenes, but the narrative is approached with a single, lackluster technique. Koontz begins each scene with a statement, either an idea, the introduction of a character or a single event, and he then proceeds to analyze that statement, however mundane. Koontz sticks to this pattern so avidly that I was able to survive the final hundred and fifty pages by reading only the first sentence of each paragraph, while reading in full those few scenes that manage to progress the limited plot. Perhaps aware of the repetitive structure, Koontz breaks off once in a while to gives us a series of brief sentences that are supposed to heighten tension, but that come across as dry and lazy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11363309626240489323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1192064018" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1192064018" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532221628p2/23841736.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1192064018?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780425218648" href="/book/show/304733.Twilight_Eyes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1192064018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8424586751676128499">Well, there's nothing quite like re-reading a favorite book only to discover that it's no longer a favorite...<br/><br/>I honestly think that this book is the reason that I love supernatural horror so much. It's been a favorite for many, many years. Unfortunately the writing is merely average, which is good normally, but I've gotten so spoiled on Koontz's later writing that average wasn't quite good enough this time. It's really amazing to see how much his writing has changed over the years. The things th</span>
  <span id="freeText8424586751676128499" style="display:none">Well, there's nothing quite like re-reading a favorite book only to discover that it's no longer a favorite...<br /><br />I honestly think that this book is the reason that I love supernatural horror so much. It's been a favorite for many, many years. Unfortunately the writing is merely average, which is good normally, but I've gotten so spoiled on Koontz's later writing that average wasn't quite good enough this time. It's really amazing to see how much his writing has changed over the years. The things that I think of as trademarks are completely absent in this book. Mainly it's humor and hope that is missing. And without humor and hope Koontz is just another author to me. Albeit one who writes some totally cool and terrifying stories. But it's the humor and hope that gives Koontz "Favorite Author" status in my mind. Anyway, it's still actually a good story.<br /><br />At the start of this book we have a 17 year old boy named Slim sneaking into a carnival in the dead of night (couldn't resist saying that), intending to get some sleep and try to join up the following day. He's been on the run for six months because he killed his uncle who was systematically murdering Slim's extended family. His uncle was what Slim calls a "goblin" . Goblins are creatures that can mask themselves as human but live to rain pain, destruction, and despair down on humans. <br /><br />He makes friends at the carnival - and actually, he's attacked by a zombie goblin at one point. I haven't seen that one before. So he makes friends at the carnival and falls madly in love with Rya Raines. Unfortunately, with his psychic mojo, he can also tell that there's going to be a goblin attack on their ferris wheel. In order to help his new friends he travels into Yontsdown to help grease the wheels for the upcoming carnival stop. In Yontsdown he finds a terrifying number of goblins and comes to realize that they're more organized than he realized.<br /><br />The attempt at a sci-fi explanation didn't work for me so I shall forever consider this to be supernatural fantasy horror, my favorite theme. Despite the demotion from my favorites shelf, this is actually a first rate tale and is entirely responsible for my love of supernatural horror stories. I've read it many times and I'll read it many more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8424586751676128499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1168562708" />
    <a title="Libbye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6678264-libbye"><img alt="Libbye" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1168562708?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Libbye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Libbye" href="/user/show/6678264-libbye">Libbye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780425238059" href="/book/show/8105271-twilight-eyes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6678264-libbye?shelf=aggressively-boring">aggressively-boring</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6678264-libbye?shelf=horny-middle-aged-men">horny-middle-aged-men</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1168562708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14775502603104353446">I read Velocity a few years ago and thought was a really interesting book. I remember disliking parts of it but not really caring because it was still compelling. Recently I saw a bunch of Koontz books for 50 cents and picked up a handful, coming away pretty excited. Twilight Eyes was the first of these I read, with really high expectations from my vague memories of Velocity. I was so sad. And frustrated. <br/>"Slim" (wtf, your name is Carl) MacKenzie is your general perfect hero, he's only 17 but he</span>
  <span id="freeText14775502603104353446" style="display:none">I read Velocity a few years ago and thought was a really interesting book. I remember disliking parts of it but not really caring because it was still compelling. Recently I saw a bunch of Koontz books for 50 cents and picked up a handful, coming away pretty excited. Twilight Eyes was the first of these I read, with really high expectations from my vague memories of Velocity. I was so sad. And frustrated. <br />"Slim" (wtf, your name is Carl) MacKenzie is your general perfect hero, he's only 17 but he's perfect so it doesn't matter and everyone tells him so. His eyes even have their own special color. He's too good for people his age and he's tragically psychic, so this strong, intelligent, mature, (we're reminded constantly how good he is at everything. I think there were two things he said he was bad at, but don't worry he was good at them when he actually did them, he's even good at hiding erections.) <br />So Slim and his tragic backstory set off for the carnival because nobody else will understand. Boohoo, sleeping on a bathroom floor for a night, so sad. Slim is excellent at everything in carnivals too, he can ring the bell with his muscles, take everyone's money (non-carnies are evil), kill the special goblins, and fix sexy &amp; sad 20-something Rya Raines by fucking her into nirvana on the side of a lake the first night. Rya Raines is cold to everyone else of course, because her tragic backstory outweighs Slim's own and she's also perfect, although her perfect comes more in "look at that tight ass", and "round boobs" and that sort of description. Conveniently her tragic backstory also makes sure that she can't be pregnant so they don't have to worry about that. The goblins turn out to be a conspiracy, oh no. Everyone sucks except them, oh no. <br /><br />Basically I thought I was going to read an interesting thriller and I ended up reading a story about a horny little pissbaby who stabs demons or something. Ew.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14775502603104353446" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Worth picking up.</span>

I had a long bread between clients one day and decided to go find something to read since I had finished another book. I had a very hard time putting this book down and I kept thinking to myself "Is this real?" that is how he writes. He talkes about real places and inputs a bazaar idea into real life. Scared me to death. Great writer. </span>

<div id="review_3629627429" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3629627429" />
    <a title="Richard K. Wilson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63635665-richard-k-wilson"><img alt="Richard K. Wilson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587104635p2/63635665.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3629627429?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard K. Wilson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard K. Wilson" href="/user/show/63635665-richard-k-wilson">Richard K. Wilson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/9586103-twilight-eyes">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who loves a good, and tense chiller!</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=creature-feature-werewolf-types">creature-feature-werewolf-types</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=disturbing-as-hell">disturbing-as-hell</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=teens-in-peril">teens-in-peril</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=carnival-horror">carnival-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=cannabalism">cannabalism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=disgust">disgust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=terrifying-and-evil">terrifying-and-evil</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63635665-richard-k-wilson?shelf=incest">incest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3629627429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12314172551450744039"><b>Still a GREAT vintage read from THE horror master; Dean R. Koontz!!! Yes, i loved this book AGAIN even after almost 40 years!</b><br/><br/>You can also check out my full video book review here on my Youtube Channel; AreYouIntoHorror? here:<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://youtu.be/EuqgkTVdA9E'>https://youtu.be/EuqgkTVdA9E</a><br/><br/>Do you ever wish you had some kind of psychic powers? Would you want to see the future, whether it be good OR bad? Well, it is the summer of 1963 and 17 year old Slim MacKenzie is on the run out of Portland OR. for murdering his horrible uncle for</span>
  <span id="freeText12314172551450744039" style="display:none"><b>Still a GREAT vintage read from THE horror master; Dean R. Koontz!!! Yes, i loved this book AGAIN even after almost 40 years!</b><br /><br />You can also check out my full video book review here on my Youtube Channel; AreYouIntoHorror? here:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://youtu.be/EuqgkTVdA9E">https://youtu.be/EuqgkTVdA9E</a><br /><br />Do you ever wish you had some kind of psychic powers? Would you want to see the future, whether it be good OR bad? Well, it is the summer of 1963 and 17 year old Slim MacKenzie is on the run out of Portland OR. for murdering his horrible uncle for sexual abuse.......and where does he end up? Right in the middle of a terrifying carnival......and Slim has twilight eyes!!! What is that, you ask? Slim can see premonitions, and he can see INSIDE people to see the evil they really are, and what he discovers; that there are 1000's of bad 'Goblins'!!! Yes, goblins. But these goblins are the worst you could ever imagine.....and imagine reading this in 1985 the year it was released, WAY before this story was copied so many ways as it is in today's Horror world of books! I did just that, then i reread it 4 more times! Then, i just finished it for the 6th time! Yes, I love this book. Koontz could write horror that was so fast paced and suspenseful you just did not realize how fast you were turning the pages!! <br /><br />When Slim comes upon the Sombra Brothers Traveling Carnival he sneaks in after the closing looking for a place to hide till the morning when he can see if he can find some kind of work. Now Slim is NOT your average 17 year old kid, he has the mind, the wonder of Life, and the wisdom of a very old soul. He comes from a family of healers and psychics, all the way from his great grand parents right thru the chain to himself......but Slim is completely different than anyone wlse is his family, he sees Goblins in the souls of humans.....and these humans are the worst of the worst. <br /><br />Slim gets a job working for the owner of many Carnie businesses and he is automatically smitten with Rya Raines, his boss. When he finds out that she is as successful as she is and she is only 21 years of age, he thinks 'i can be in her position if I really want to'. Well, Rya hires him to run the 'ring the bell at the top of the pole' game, I cannot for the life of me remember right now what Rya called it....lol. Damn! When on the night of his first day of work, he witnesses a murder of a man, by someone that he realizes is a goblin, and when he goes to find the body that night after the carnival shuts down....it is no where to be found. This is when he meets and befriends one of the 'Freaks from the Human Oddities' show, the 3 eyed man. Yes, these people in this carnival are all real nothing faked here, and this adds to the story so much, that you actually start to think these things could actually be real and or happen! <br /><br />With this book being 451 pages, it is broken down into 2 parts, the first taking place in the carnival, and the 2nd part taking place across America with Slim and Rya tracking down and trying to stop and kill the entire Goblins race.......good luck to this attempt!! This is still in my opinion Koontz's best book right behind his 'The Funhouse' and 'Voice In The Night' which i absolutely LOVED both of these books......This was when Koontz could really write incredibly scary novels that stuck with you forever, and since he now writes what I call suspenseful thrillers, if you love HORROR of the intense and very different kind, pick this vintage classic up!!! Highly recommended.<br /><br />4.5 Stabbies!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12314172551450744039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=own-in-paperback">own-in-paperback</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2010">read-in-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103108330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11481395705011752927">"Twilight Eyes" was originally published as a limited edition in 1985. In 1987 Koontz expanded the story by writing the second part and published it again...which unfortunately shows.<br/><br/>Part 1 is interesting: Slim McKenzie, A 17-year old boy who sees monsters he names "goblins" wanders into a carnival to find peace. These Goblins aren't your ordinary monsters - they hide under a human guise. But Slim has Twilight Eyes, a powr which allows him to see through the curtain and kill the wicked creature </span>
  <span id="freeText11481395705011752927" style="display:none">"Twilight Eyes" was originally published as a limited edition in 1985. In 1987 Koontz expanded the story by writing the second part and published it again...which unfortunately shows.<br /><br />Part 1 is interesting: Slim McKenzie, A 17-year old boy who sees monsters he names "goblins" wanders into a carnival to find peace. These Goblins aren't your ordinary monsters - they hide under a human guise. But Slim has Twilight Eyes, a powr which allows him to see through the curtain and kill the wicked creature beneath.<br /><br />I usually don't care for Koontz's characters, but this book is a bit different that his others. Slim sounds like someone I could possibly believe is 17, and his love interest is interesting and mysterious. There are cheesy love scenes and corny dialogues between the two (the girl climbs a stairway of climaxes. Get it ? A stairway of climaxes. Shame on you Led Zep) but overall the atmosphere is engrossing enough to make you turn the page.<br />I liked the fact that Slim was presented as unreliable, and it all could have happened inside his head - making him a bloody murderer. However, since this is a Koontz book it's obviously not the case and we have a god guy battling the evil monsters. Part 1 ends in an extremely anticlimatic way, but still the ride is interesting enough to guarantee at least some degree of satisfaction.<br /><br />Part 2 sucks balls. Koontz strips his characters from the shreds of personality he gave them, takes away their voices and insserts his own political mumbo jumbo. Obviously some writers use express their views in their novels, but he is so obvious that it hurts. An example follows:<br /><br />"As the news ended, Rya switched off the radio and said, "Not all the<br />evil in the world comes from the goblins."<br />"No."<br />"We're capable of our own atrocities."<br />"Very capable," I agreed.<br />She was silent for a moment, listening to distant cries of sea gulls<br />and to the gentle waves breaking softly on the shore.<br />At last she said, "Year by year, through the death and suffering and<br />cruelty that the goblins produce, they force goodness and honesty and<br />truth into an ever smaller corner.<br />We live in a world that grows colder and meaner all the time, Mostly though not entirely-because of them, a world in which most of the examples of behavior for younger generations are increasingly bad examples. Which guarantees that each new generation will be less compassionate than the one before it. Each new generation will have a greater tolerance for lies and murder and cruelty. We're less than twenty years removed from Hitler's mass murders, but do most people seem to remember or care what happened? Stalin killed at least three times as many as Hitler, but no one speaks of it.<br />Now, in China, Mao Tse-tung is killing millions and grinding millions more to dust in slave-labor camps, but do you hear many cries of outrage? The trend won't be reversed until <br />"Until?"<br />"Until we do something about the goblins.""<br /><br />Amd other stuff like this, including the emphasis of love and friendship, something hedoes all the time in most blalant ways. Part 2 is long, drawn out, boring as hell with another anticlimatic ending which is forced and unsatysfying.<br /><br />When you can tell that the author speaks through his puppet, it means it's a bad book. But I have a soft spot for most of Koontz's books, so I give this one three stars. If you want to read it, you can safely skip part two - it took me three days to get through part one and seven to finish the second.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11481395705011752927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13012698812953952675">Dean Koontz has created an epic back history in Twilight Eyes, much like he did with Phantoms. The lead characters bring to mind Odd and Stormy who will years later be the feature love story in Dean's most well-known work, Odd Thomas. Like Odd Thomas, the character of Slim McKenzie is connected to the carnival setting and has the paranormal ability to see things that most people can not. It seems apparent that Dean left the story of Twilight Eyes open for a possible sequel. <br/><br/>Favorite Passages:<i><br/><br/>Eve</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13012698812953952675" style="display:none">Dean Koontz has created an epic back history in Twilight Eyes, much like he did with Phantoms. The lead characters bring to mind Odd and Stormy who will years later be the feature love story in Dean's most well-known work, Odd Thomas. Like Odd Thomas, the character of Slim McKenzie is connected to the carnival setting and has the paranormal ability to see things that most people can not. It seems apparent that Dean left the story of Twilight Eyes open for a possible sequel. <br /><br />Favorite Passages:<i><br /><br />Even for those of us fortunate enough to avoid the goblins, life is not entirely smooth and easy. But the myraid methods we employ to get ourselves across patches of rough ground are a testament to our great will to survive and to get on with the act of living.<br />______<br /><br />Life, as I might have said before, is a long train ride during which friends and loved ones disembark unexpectedly, leaving us to continue our travels in ever-increasing loneliness. Here was another station on the line. </i></span>

<div id="review_3495361834" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3495361834" />
    <a title="Dave Edmunds" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/93688875-dave-edmunds"><img alt="Dave Edmunds" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570659900p2/93688875.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3495361834?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave Edmunds" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave Edmunds" href="/user/show/93688875-dave-edmunds">Dave Edmunds</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3495361834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11926620329951454077">For years I have banged on about how Intensity is Dean Koontz's best novel. That is until I read this fantastic piece of horror fiction.<br/><br/>Twilight Eyes is a phenomenal read from start to finish. Split into two parts, the first part is set within a carnival, where the protagonist (Slim Mackenzie) battles against a demonic force whilst on the run from the law. It's absolutely superb and definitely the best part, with brilliant description and some fantastic characters.<br/><br/>The second part is not too shab</span>
  <span id="freeText11926620329951454077" style="display:none">For years I have banged on about how Intensity is Dean Koontz's best novel. That is until I read this fantastic piece of horror fiction.<br /><br />Twilight Eyes is a phenomenal read from start to finish. Split into two parts, the first part is set within a carnival, where the protagonist (Slim Mackenzie) battles against a demonic force whilst on the run from the law. It's absolutely superb and definitely the best part, with brilliant description and some fantastic characters.<br /><br />The second part is not too shabby either and provides a satisfying conclusion and plenty of action. If there's one thing Koontz knows how to do it's pace his novels and he's absolutely on point in this one. There's one scene in a house with a basement that had me on the edge of my seat. Intense stuff!<br /><br />So all in all, I have a new favourite Koontz novel. I've read some good ones but Twilight Eyes is definitely a standout. If you're looking to get into reading some Koontz this is a perfect place to start.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11926620329951454077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21670666" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21670666" />
    <a title="Brett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/436346-brett"><img alt="Brett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195094919p2/436346.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21670666?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brett" href="/user/show/436346-brett">Brett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/436346-brett?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21670666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18255876571576848708">Twilight Eyes is a better than average Dean Koontz novel, but still not a very good book. I was sort intrigued at the outset--the writing is a little more solid than most Koontz books and initially he did a fair job of allowing us to see of the interior doubts of the main character. At first the narrator, referred to as Slim, does not seem reliable, which is very unusual for Koontz, as he usually deals only in characters that are purely good or purely evil (one of the reasons why his books are o</span>
  <span id="freeText18255876571576848708" style="display:none">Twilight Eyes is a better than average Dean Koontz novel, but still not a very good book. I was sort intrigued at the outset--the writing is a little more solid than most Koontz books and initially he did a fair job of allowing us to see of the interior doubts of the main character. At first the narrator, referred to as Slim, does not seem reliable, which is very unusual for Koontz, as he usually deals only in characters that are purely good or purely evil (one of the reasons why his books are often so boring). Slim believes he can see "goblins" which take the form of people and walk among us. He has killed several people he believes are goblins, including braining his uncle with an ax. Of course, the reader is left with some doubt about Slim's ability to actually see goblins, as opposed to simply being crazy.<br /><br />Well, Koontz doesn't let that go on for long. He establishes that goblins are real and Slim can see them, taking away the dramatic tension he started to build. The first half of the novel takes place mostly against the backdrop of a travelling carnival (the second carnival book I've read this year, after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43641.Water_for_Elephants" title="Water for Elephants by Sara Gruen" rel="nofollow noopener">Water for Elephants</a>). The first section is the strongest section; part two is slightly longer than part one, but feels like it was tacked on as an afterthought. Here we find out the stupid and uninteresting backstory of how the goblins came into being and we follow Slim and his girlfriend in their war against the goblins. Both Slim and his girlfriend have some kind psychic abilities, another crutch Koontz regularly uses to help his characters solve problems when he writes himself into a corner. <br /><br />After early high hopes, this book is another disappointment from Koontz. Plagued by bad characterization, worse dialogue, and flaky plot. Not recommended for anyone.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18255876571576848708" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2819716078" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2819716078" />
    <a title="Pritam Majumder  TheBookAddict" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/89498614-pritam-majumder-thebookaddict"><img alt="Pritam Majumder  TheBookAddict" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583091887p2/89498614.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2819716078?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pritam Majumder  TheBookAddict" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pritam Majumder  TheBookAddict" href="/user/show/89498614-pritam-majumder-thebookaddict">Pritam Majumder  TheBookAddict</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747235170" href="/book/show/903224.Twilight_Eyes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/89498614-pritam-majumder-thebookaddict?shelf=dean-koontz">dean-koontz</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2819716078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6526430593814339810">It's a classic Dean Koontz book. This story is actually divided by two parts. In first part we see the protagonist of the story slim MacKenzie who can see goblins by his twilight eyes. The goblins are the monsters who lives among humans by disguising one of them. But slim can see there real goblin looks through their disguise. And he hunts them. In that course he comes up a carnival called sombra brothers where he meet with Rya. But things are becoming difficult for him when he started to see so</span>
  <span id="freeText6526430593814339810" style="display:none">It's a classic Dean Koontz book. This story is actually divided by two parts. In first part we see the protagonist of the story slim MacKenzie who can see goblins by his twilight eyes. The goblins are the monsters who lives among humans by disguising one of them. But slim can see there real goblin looks through their disguise. And he hunts them. In that course he comes up a carnival called sombra brothers where he meet with Rya. But things are becoming difficult for him when he started to see so many goblins in the fair ground. He needs to know what goblins are after in that carnival. On second half of this story we see slim can know much about the goblins and he decided to struck them in greater way. And the story shows us very dark truth about goblins. <br />In my opinion the first half of this story is very fun ride. The entire carnival part is paced very smoothly and also gives creeps us with all goblin encounter with slim. <br />On second part the story become very darker and sometimes it's made me depressed. All the fun of its first part is missing here. But this part gives us more chills and goosebumps. <br />Finally I can say I enjoyed it a lot. And I must thanks Edward Lorn (our beloved E) to suggest this book on his YouTube video.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6526430593814339810" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_311513436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/311513436" />
    <a title="Michelle Weber" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8731022-michelle-weber"><img alt="Michelle Weber" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340989319p2/8731022.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/311513436?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle Weber" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle Weber" href="/user/show/8731022-michelle-weber">Michelle Weber</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer311513436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1856925286560952488">I've read a number of Koontz' novels and this one is by far the worst one I have read. His books usually keep me turning pages, but this book took me over a year to finish. I wasn't too fond of Slim calling the genetically altered war weapons as "goblins" and that's one of the many reasons I couldn't take this book seriously. DK sometimes gets carried away with his descriptions, but this time he took it too far. It took him far, far too long to get to the point and I lost interest several times.</span>
  <span id="freeText1856925286560952488" style="display:none">I've read a number of Koontz' novels and this one is by far the worst one I have read. His books usually keep me turning pages, but this book took me over a year to finish. I wasn't too fond of Slim calling the genetically altered war weapons as "goblins" and that's one of the many reasons I couldn't take this book seriously. DK sometimes gets carried away with his descriptions, but this time he took it too far. It took him far, far too long to get to the point and I lost interest several times. The only reason I didn't give up on it completely is because I have OCD and can't start something without finishing it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1856925286560952488" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It's different because the mosters are goblins posing as humans who want to destroy mankind. I also love the fact that the characters are in a traveling carnival. A part of me would like to live that life for a while and this gives it to me vicariously. lol</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yyDZJhudbWchCXCX+Iw5DiO9TAn+RicVs8XYf1updxa3vo4KwZxNYWuV3qmZRlBV/6HF7a1RoCx79COfKWAIgw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cJJc9CIr4M4Ig2VkMUf9Hn4lzKRRfQF34D3eS0TrrgUMDAvY+CrAyEIfy1pQjZRFojlFQAJqhk4oDCWrNiLRkA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Here, one hundred years ago, on the night of the annual August Moon Ball, a shy and lovely servant girl committed a single, unspeakable act of violence—an act so shocking its legacy lives still.\n\nAnd now, long after the horror of that night has faded to a tale whispered by children around summer camp fires, an unholy terror is about to be reborn.\n\nNow, one family is about to feel the icy hand of supernatural fear—as Melissa Holloway, shy and troubled and just thirteen years old, comes to know the blood-drenched secret that waits behind a locked attic door... For in the dead of night a Secret Cove sleeps unaware, a soul-chilling presence slowly begins to enact a terrifying vengeance.\n\nSecond Child: It is unspeakable evil merely Melissa\'s nightmares made horrifyingly real? Is it the manifestation of deadly fury risen from the grave?   <span id="freeText18085896945659929933" style="display:none">Acknowledged as "America's most popular suspense novelist" (Rolling Stone) and as one of today's most celebrated and successful writers, Dean Ray Koontz has earned the devotion of millions of readers around the world and the praise of critics everywhere for tales of character, mystery, and adventure that strike to the core of what it means to be human.<br /><br />Dean, the author of many #1 New York Times bestsellers, lives in Southern California with his wife, Gerda, their golden retriever, Elsa, and the enduring spirit of their goldens, Trixie and Anna.<br /> <br />Facebook: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://Facebook.com/DeanKoontzOfficial">Facebook.com/DeanKoontzOfficial</a><br />Twitter: @DeanKoontz<br />Website: DeanKoontz.com </span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/442715-twilight-eyes">Quotes from Twilight Eyes</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Oh, we all are, my young friend! We all deceive. Some of us deceive the whole world, every single fellow creature we meet. Some of us deceive only selected people, wives and lovers, or mothers and fathers. And some of us deceive only ourselves. But none of us is totally honest with everyone all the time, in all matters. Hell, the need to deceive is just one more curse that our sorry species has to bear.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1153345-oh-we-all-are-my-young-friend-we-all-deceive">6 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Hope is a constant companion in this life. It is the one thing that neither cruel nature, God, nor other men can wrench from us. Health, wealth, beloved brothers and sisters, children, friends, the past, the future - all can be stolen from us as easily as an unguarded purse. But our greatest treasure, hope, remains. It is a sturdy little motor within, purring, ticking, driving us on when reason would suggest surrender. It is both the most pathetic and noblest thing about us, the most absurd and the most admirable quality we possess, for as long as we have hope, we also have the capacity for love, for caring, for decency.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/7423693-hope-is-a-constant-companion-in-this-life-it-is">4 likes</a>
            </nobr>
